    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1080885451" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1080885451" />
    <a title="Ferdy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4596358-ferdy"><img alt="Ferdy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1487898103p2/4596358.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1080885451?book_show_action=true">Oct 15, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ferdy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ferdy" href="/user/show/4596358-ferdy">Ferdy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4596358-ferdy?shelf=multiple-pov">multiple-pov</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4596358-ferdy?shelf=liked-the-world">liked-the-world</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4596358-ferdy?shelf=evul-scientists">evul-scientists</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4596358-ferdy?shelf=liked-it">liked-it</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4596358-ferdy?shelf=teens-saving-the-world">teens-saving-the-world</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4596358-ferdy?shelf=good">good</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4596358-ferdy?shelf=dystopian">dystopian</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4596358-ferdy?shelf=evul-corporations">evul-corporations</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4596358-ferdy?shelf=evul-people-are-evul">evul-people-are-evul</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4596358-ferdy?shelf=young-adult">young-adult</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1080885451" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5479983818477840320"><b>3.5 stars - Spoilers</b><br/><br/>I didn't think it would be possible but I've finally read a decent ending to a dystopian series. <i>Undivided</i> was by no means perfect but unlike other YA dystopia there was no nonsensical or rubbish resolutions, everything made sense within the context of the world. Sure, some parts were rushed and predictable but there was no WTFery or flipping back and forth to try and make sense of what was going on. Pretty much all the various plot arcs were resolved in a satisfying way, whi</span>
  <span id="freeText5479983818477840320" style="display:none"><b>3.5 stars - Spoilers</b><br /><br />I didn't think it would be possible but I've finally read a decent ending to a dystopian series. <i>Undivided</i> was by no means perfect but unlike other YA dystopia there was no nonsensical or rubbish resolutions, everything made sense within the context of the world. Sure, some parts were rushed and predictable but there was no WTFery or flipping back and forth to try and make sense of what was going on. Pretty much all the various plot arcs were resolved in a satisfying way, which for a dystopia is rather brilliant.<br /><br />-Summary: Everyone age 13-18 can be Unwound (killed/all their organs donated). Government and various corporations are all evil and greedy, they want Unwinding to forever be successful as organ business makes them lots of money. Most of society cool with misbehaving kids being Unwound as they want their homes/towns/families to be safe from naughty children. Naturally, the Unwind kids not cool with their death. This unsurprisingly leads to a bunch of plucky teens rebelling against society/government/corporations. <br />Plucky teens, Connor/Risa/Lev, all trying to stop Unwinding and change the law. Loads of evil/powerful people trying to stop them, the fuckers.<br /><br />-Some parts were rather predictable, it was obvious Proactive Citizenry would be behind the clapper movement, and the Starkey/stork storyline played out pretty much exactly as I expected, and Argent's arc was utterly predictable up until he met Conner again.<br /><br />-One of the best parts was Conner's unwinding scenes, it was tense and shocking. I didn't think he would make it, and I loved how Argent/Risa/the Admiral all worked together to save him.<br />The scene where various parents were getting letters from their unwound kids was good too, especially the one with Roland's mum - I'm glad the cow will suffer not knowing for certain if he survived. <br />I found Lev's arc engrossing too, him getting tattoos of various Unwind kids and sacrificing himself to make a statement was quite powerful. I loved Miracolina's interaction with him later on, they were so cute together. <br />My most favourite part was Nelson getting Unwound, it deserved the bastard right, Argent redeemed himself somewhat by taking him down.<br /><br />-I wasn't a fan of Conner until later in the book, he was so shouty and moody. His POV before he was captured by Nelson kind of ruined my enjoyment at times. It was as if he thought he was the only one suffering, all the other teen characters were going through the same thing but they didn't act like jerks. I wished he'd saved some of his dick behaviour for his parents instead of constantly snapping at Risa. <br /><br />-Risa didn't seem to have any agency of her own in this one, she was there just to worry about all things Conner and didn't have her own purpose. Yes, she wanted to change things and get the 3D organ printer out there, but it was all secondary to Conner. I wanted her to fight for the Unwinds and take a stand but all she did was stay in the background whilst Conner had all the drive and purpose. She had more depth and personality in earlier books, she was just the love interest in this one.<br /><br />-I was unbelievably glad most of the bad guys ended up dead or suffering. <br />I was pissed Divan got away scot free though, he was just as evil as Nelson and Roberta but nothing bad happened to him. I suppose it would have been unrealistic if all the bad guys got their comeuppance. I'm hoping Divan and Bah Zhey or whatever they were called ended up killing each other in painful ways.<br /><br />-There were a lot of likeable and engaging characters, and they all had their own journey/purpose. Grace was awesome, she was my fave. I loved how she handled the 3D organ printer thing, she made sure to give it to the right person and she managed to secure her own future too, it was brilliant.<br />Bam and Hayden were great as well, them working together to take down Starkey was such fun to read. I wanted Starkey to suffer for creating so much death and mayhem, but when he ended up being Unwound/harvested, I felt so sorry for him, his craziness was mostly a product of his parents/society, he wasn't pure evil like the adults in the novel. Even though he was awful, he never really had a chance to be anything other than horrid so him suffering as much as he did was kind of upsetting.<br />Una's arc was quite depressing, her wedding to 'Wil' was weird and sad. I liked her accepting and helping Cam at the end though, it was good they both had each other for comfort and company.<br /><br />-I HATED the ending, it pissed me off. Well, I didn't actually hate the ending except for the part where Conner was all lovey-dovey and huggy with his parents - the parents who willingly signed his death order. Why did his parents sign him up for Unwinding/death? Because he wasn't the perfect son. Had he done anything evil or unforgivable or dangerous? No, he was a normal teenager who had ups and downs, and that was enough for his heartless parents to send him to his death. They knew exactly what they were doing, they knew they'd be betraying and hurting their son in the worst possible way, they knew full well Conner would no longer exist but they did it anyway because they didn't love him and they wanted holiday money in exchange for his body parts. <br />It was good of Conner to save his parents from being killed by a murderous crowd, it showed how much of a better person he was than them. But I wasn't impressed with him holding them like everything was fine and dandy, and then him thinking he would one day forgive them was just bonkers. After what they did to him, he should have told them he would never forgive them. Conner could have still had his brother as family, he didn't need his cruel backstabbing parents in his life leeching of his fame and goodness. <br />The only reason his parents even turned up at the rally in the end was because society as a whole was finally starting to condemn unwinding and they jumped on the bandwagon. Also it was because Conner was a famous hero and they thought they had a right to act like the proud, grieving parents. If Conner was still seen as a criminal and if unwinding was still seen as a good thing, his parents would have been glad he was dead. They only wanted Conner's forgiveness, because they didn't want to feel bad or be seen as the bad guys, it wasn't for his sake. They were irredeemable, I wish Conner had told them what awful parents they were and walked away from them, they needed to be punished in some way.<br /><br />-Didn't mind the multiple POV's as much in this one, it didn't work as well in the last book but it was done well this time.<br /><br />This somewhat redeemed the dystopia genre for me, I'm still not a fan but I no longer loathe it. The <i>Unwind Dystology</i> as a whole shows that well written, thought provoking, entertaining dystopian can be written.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="5479983818477840320" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer13139225195775658075">What a great ending to a creepy and thought-provoking dystopian series where teens ages 13-17 can now be "unwound" or chopped up for body parts if their parents consent. I really loved the way Neal Shusterman wrapped up the arcs for each of our main characters, Connor, Risa, and Lev, and also the way that he dove into the history of how such a world could have evolved. It seems far-fetched, and probably wouldn't ever become a widespread reality, but certainly makes for a scary what-if. <br/><br/>This was </span>
  <span id="freeText13139225195775658075" style="display:none">What a great ending to a creepy and thought-provoking dystopian series where teens ages 13-17 can now be "unwound" or chopped up for body parts if their parents consent. I really loved the way Neal Shusterman wrapped up the arcs for each of our main characters, Connor, Risa, and Lev, and also the way that he dove into the history of how such a world could have evolved. It seems far-fetched, and probably wouldn't ever become a widespread reality, but certainly makes for a scary what-if. <br /><br />This was a particularly satisfying ending to a series <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ because we got to see our main three characters earn their happy endings, even if they weren't perfect endings. Connor was unwound and will have to recover from that. Una and Cam have a long road ahead. Lev has wounds he'll have to live with, but they all completed the arcs they were meant to and found a satisfying end. As did bad characters, even if that end was somewhat grisly (won't go there.)  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> All in all, this was an excellent series, highly recommended. 4.5/5 stars.</span>

<div id="review_688447712" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/688447712" />
    <a title="Wendy F" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4019599-wendy-f"><img alt="Wendy F" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1486662406p2/4019599.jpg" /></a>

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/688447712?book_show_action=true">Aug 07, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Wendy F" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Wendy F" href="/user/show/4019599-wendy-f">Wendy F</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4019599-wendy-f?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4019599-wendy-f?shelf=2014">2014</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4019599-wendy-f?shelf=neal-shusterman">neal-shusterman</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer688447712" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18386942864302422488">This is it. The final book in the Unwind dystology. Dystology… my computer says this isn’t even a word. Which I suppose is fair, since Unwind, Rewound, and Storking aren't words either. It fits. A ‘Dystopian-ology’.<br/><br/>Anyway, I read Unwind quite a while ago, before even book 2 was out back when Unwind was supposed to be just a standalone but the fans fell in love with the story of the Heartland War and the Unwind accord and Shusterman decided to turn it into a series instead. The ride over the cour</span>
  <span id="freeText18386942864302422488" style="display:none">This is it. The final book in the Unwind dystology. Dystology… my computer says this isn’t even a word. Which I suppose is fair, since Unwind, Rewound, and Storking aren't words either. It fits. A ‘Dystopian-ology’.<br /><br />Anyway, I read Unwind quite a while ago, before even book 2 was out back when Unwind was supposed to be just a standalone but the fans fell in love with the story of the Heartland War and the Unwind accord and Shusterman decided to turn it into a series instead. The ride over the course of the 4.5 books was both up and down, some better than others. In one book I would dislike a character, and in another I would love him. (This is a good explanation for my feelings on Lev Calder/Garrity.)<br /><br />In UnDivided, the final book in the Dystology, I was prepared to be let down. Usually in a final book the anticipation is so high that the book can’t measure up. You feel like something is missing, or glossed over, or rushed through. I thought it likely that I would just read it and then move on, unaffected. The reality is that I don’t think it was the best I've read, but it did make a statement. At about 75% the tears were rolling down my face, and it continued that way off and on until the end of the book. So much sacrifice. I don’t want to give anything away, just take away from this review that it was so good. It was worth it all.<br /><br />One thing, you know how other series where the novella’s and shorts are accents but don’t necessarily need to be read to enjoy the main series? The Unwind Dystology is not like that. There is one short. It comes after book one, before book 2, and you really should make sure you read it. You can read 2 and enjoy it, though there are some undertones you won’t understand. You can’t read books 3 and the final book 4 if you haven’t read Unstrung. The events in Unstrung have a very big influence on what happens in those final books.<br /><br />Unique, wonderful, dystopian series.<br /><br /><b>There are plenty of great reviews, just like this one, over at <a href="http://badassbookreviews.com/" rel="nofollow noopener">Badass Book Reviews</a>! Check us out!</b></span>

          </span>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10378346-louisa?shelf=young-adult">young-adult</a>,

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10378346-louisa?shelf=dystopian-utopian">dystopian-utopian</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10378346-louisa?shelf=2014-favourites">2014-favourites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer745129077" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14943161647164766879">WHAT A RIDE. WHAT A SERIES. WHAT A CONCLUSION. WHAT A ROLLERCOASTER OF EMOTIONS. Brb need to eat some lobster to calm myself down :')</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3511508651" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3511508651" />
    <a title="Bradley" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4213258-bradley"><img alt="Bradley" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1508811508p2/4213258.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3511508651?book_show_action=true">Sep 06, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bradley" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bradley" href="/user/show/4213258-bradley">Bradley</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4213258-bradley?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4213258-bradley?shelf=dystopia-yes-pls">dystopia-yes-pls</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4213258-bradley?shelf=ya">ya</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4213258-bradley?shelf=2020-shelf">2020-shelf</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3511508651" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4091960649228476525">Well, now that the series is done, I have to re-order my expectations. <br/><br/>Did I expect a bigger, more creative, and crazy end? Possibly. Did I expect a revolution? Yes.<br/><br/>Was I satisfied with the whole perception of reality resolution and the legal niceties and sacrifices? Yes.<br/><br/>So what's my problem? The YA conforms to all the general YA standards. The kids are given horrible situations but not TOO horrible situations. It's mostly dealing with their own expectations and plodding their way toward changin</span>
  <span id="freeText4091960649228476525" style="display:none">Well, now that the series is done, I have to re-order my expectations. <br /><br />Did I expect a bigger, more creative, and crazy end? Possibly. Did I expect a revolution? Yes.<br /><br />Was I satisfied with the whole perception of reality resolution and the legal niceties and sacrifices? Yes.<br /><br />So what's my problem? The YA conforms to all the general YA standards. The kids are given horrible situations but not TOO horrible situations. It's mostly dealing with their own expectations and plodding their way toward changing everyone ELSE's expectations. It didn't become a horror masterpiece with tons of shambling Cthulhu monstrosities of added limbs with full personalities active in every limb. We didn't have a full society of rejects or all the old unwound kids rebelling WITHIN all the bodies they were transplanted to. Alas. That would have been brilliant.<br /><br />Instead, we have legal bills, special interests, public perception, and protests.<br /><br /><br />Not that I have anything against protests. Far from it.<br /><br />I mean, the whole idea that any society could be set up to drive another segment of its population crazy is not very crazy at all. I mean, with all the destruction of human rights and dignity going on, we really SHOULDN'T be surprised that the disenfranchised are so upset.<br /><br />Of course, to blame that same population for being angry after having been beaten down for so long and initiate draconian measures against them is pretty much a Fascist Playbook kind of thing.<br /><br /><br />Unfortunately, when I look at society now and compare it with the mildness of these books, I think this is a case of reality being stranger than fiction. Or at least wilder.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4091960649228476525" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('geU0Rq1izE8rBzDZKzbWZBpeQgbcGb7woXpXR03mts+NI53OgO1jM9k08Z4fC4p0eey5h/omBLQqvX2Z6G1Gaw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('rkjnHgG2gYKjPnCXuQwfoxxgGfTOZbxnVY8srE6CgByijk6WLDku/lENsdCNMUOzf9LidehaBiPeSAZy6wlwuA==')})
  }
//]]>

      <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/19564.Neal_Shusterman">About Neal Shusterman</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/19564.Neal_Shusterman"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1246977170p3/19564.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/19564.Neal_Shusterman">Neal Shusterman
</a></div>
<div class='goodreadsAuthorBadge'></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
18,559
followers

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer6379980989578047683">Award-winning author Neal Shusterman grew up in Brooklyn, New York, where he began writing at an early age. After spending his junior and senior years of high school at the American School of Mexico City, Neal went on to UC Irvine, where he made his mark on the UCI swim team, and wrote a successful humor column. Within a year of graduating, he had his first book deal, and was hired to write a movi</span>
  <span id="freeText6379980989578047683" style="display:none">Award-winning author Neal Shusterman grew up in Brooklyn, New York, where he began writing at an early age. After spending his junior and senior years of high school at the American School of Mexico City, Neal went on to UC Irvine, where he made his mark on the UCI swim team, and wrote a successful humor column. Within a year of graduating, he had his first book deal, and was hired to write a movie script.<br /><br />In the years since, Neal has made his mark as a successful novelist, screenwriter, and television writer. As a full-time writer, he claims to be his own hardest task-master, always at work creating new stories to tell. His books have received many awards from organizations such as the International Reading Association, and the American Library Association, as well as garnering a myriad of state and local awards across the country. Neal's talents range from film directing (two short films he directed won him the coveted CINE Golden Eagle Awards) to writing music and stage plays – including book and lyrical contributions to “American Twistory,” which is currently playing in Boston. He has even tried his hand at creating Games, having developed three successful "How to Host a Mystery" game for teens, as well as seven "How to Host a Murder" games.<br /><br />As a screen and TV writer, Neal has written for the "Goosebumps" and “Animorphs” TV series, and wrote the Disney Channel Original Movie “Pixel Perfect”. Currently Neal is adapting his novel Everlost as a feature film for Universal Studios.<br /><br />Wherever Neal goes, he quickly earns a reputation as a storyteller and dynamic speaker. Much of his fiction is traceable back to stories he tells to large audiences of children and teenagers -- such as his novel The Eyes of Kid Midas. As a speaker, Neal is in constant demand at schools and conferences. Degrees in both psychology and drama give Neal a unique approach to writing. Neal's novels always deal with topics that appeal to adults as well as teens, weaving true-to-life characters into sensitive and riveting issues, and binding it all together with a unique and entertaining sense of humor.<br /><br />Of Everlost, School Library Journal wrote: “Shusterman has reimagined what happens after death and questions power and the meaning of charity. While all this is going on, he has also managed to write a rip-roaring adventure…”<br /><br />Of What Daddy Did, Voice of Youth Advocates wrote; "This is a compelling, spell-binding story... A stunning novel, impossible to put down once begun.<br /><br />Of The Schwa Was Here, School Library Journal wrote: “Shusterman's characters–reminiscent of those crafted by E. L. Konigsburg and Jerry Spinelli–are infused with the kind of controlled, precocious improbability that magically vivifies the finest children's classics.<br /><br />Of Scorpion Shards, Publisher's Weekly wrote: "Shusterman takes an outlandish comic-book concept, and, through the sheer audacity and breadth of his imagination makes it stunningly believable. A spellbinder."<br /><br />And of The Eyes of Kid Midas, The Midwest Book Review wrote "This wins our vote as one of the best young-adult titles of the year" and was called "Inspired and hypnotically readable" by School Library Journal.<br /><br />Neal Shusterman lives in Southern California with his children Brendan, Jarrod, Joelle, and Erin, who are a constant source of inspiration!</span>
  <a data-text-id="6379980989578047683" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
